<a id="paper-2605.26336"></a>
### [Cylindrical Trap Dependence in the Unitary Fermi Gas](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26336v1)

**Authors:** Adèle Le Borgne  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26336v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26336_figures/2605.26336_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1 – Condensate confined in cylinder-shape geometry, via a power law potential in the transverse direction and by end caps in the longitudinal direction. There is no potential along z; the solutions are plane waves with frequency depending on the boundary condi- tions.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper extends the theoretical description of a unitary Fermi gas from spherical to cylindrical trap geometries. It demonstrates how the interplay between longitudinal and transverse confinement modifies the dynamic structure factor. These results are crucial for accurately interpreting experimental measurements of density fluctuations in trapped quantum gases.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides essential corrections for interpreting Bragg spectroscopy data in ultracold atom experiments, specifically regarding how trap anisotropy breaks isotropy and modifies the phonon dispersion relation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the challenge of interpreting experimental spectra in trapped unitary Fermi gases, specifically how cylindrical trap geometries modify the dynamic structure factor and phonon dispersion relation.

**Main result.** The study derives how cylindrical confinement modifies the dynamic structure factor, finding that longitudinal confinement contributes a convex correction while transverse confinement contributes a concave correction, with the net effect being convex for typical experimental parameters.

**Method.** The authors use an Effective Field Theory (EFT) framework based on superfluid hydrodynamics, employing a Wentzel-Kramers-Brillouin (WKB) expansion and a hierarchy of scales approach to analyze different energy regimes.

**Model / system.** A unitary Fermi gas (a strongly coupled, nonrelativistic Conformal Field Theory) realized using ultracold 6Li atoms, confined in a cylindrical geometry with power-law radial potentials and axial light sheets.

**Key observables.** Dynamic structure factor S(q, omega), phonon dispersion relation, and the energy spectrum.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bertsch parameter (xi), speed of sound (cs), dispersion parameter (gamma), aspect ratio (epsilon), and low-energy constants (c0, c1, c2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is treated in the zero-temperature limit, the radial potential is assumed to approximate a hard-wall box, and the Bragg pulse is assumed to be in the linear response regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the condensate in cylindrical geometry; Figure 2 shows the energy spectrum and the accuracy of the WKB fit using numerical diagonalization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of trap-induced distortions, extends a spherical EFT framework to cylindrical geometry, identifies three physical energy regimes, derives the modified dynamic structure factor, and compares analytical WKB results with numerical diagonalization.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The unitary Fermi gas serves as a tunable realization of a strongly coupled CFT, making it a powerful system for probing universal quantum many-body phenomena. Precise measurement of its properties remains experimentally challenging: finite-temperature effects and spatial inhomogeneity introduced by external trapping potentials can significantly distort observables. Cylindrical trap geometries are commonly used in experiments. This note extends an existing theoretical framework to this geometry, deriving how the cylindrical confinement modifies the dynamic structure factor at zero temperature. These results provide a necessary correction for the interpretation of experimental spectra in trapped unitary gases.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.26261"></a>
### [GPU-accelerated finite-temperature Lanczos method for spin Hamiltonians](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26261v1)

**Authors:** Shadan Ghassemi Tabrizi, Thomas D. Kühne  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26261v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26261_figures/2605.26261_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Thermodynamic observables and precision error for the antiferromagnetic icosahedron with local spin s = 1 (panels a, b) or s = 3/2 (c, d); left: heat capacity C, right: magnetic susceptibility χ. FP64 (solid blue) and FP32 (dashed red) are plotted on the linear left y-axis; both types of curves coincide at the resolution of the plot. The pointwise precision error, |ΔC| = |CFP32 − CFP64| or |Δχ| = |χFP32 − χFP64|, is plotted in black against its logarithmic right y-axis.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26261_figures/2605.26261_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Multi-seed stochastic error analysis for the heat capacity and magnetic susceptibility of the icosahedron with s = 1 (a, b, c, d) or s = 3/2 (e, f, g, h). Left: 50 independent FTLM estimates (red curves, R = 100 each) and the pooled Reff = 5000 results (blue). Right: empirical and theoretical standard deviations across the 50 runs. See main text for details.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26261_figures/2605.26261_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Thermodynamic observables and precision error (for details, see caption to Figure 1) for the s = 1, N = 12 Heisenberg ring (panels a, b), the s = 1/2 , N = 20 ring (c, d), the s = 1/2 dodecahedron (e, f) and the s = 1/2 icosidodecahedron (g). For the icosidodecahedron the calculation was restricted to the M = 0 sector (thus χ = 0, not shown) and only R = 8 random vectors were used.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: Ghost diagnostic for the Lanczos Ritz spectrum – based on the same random starting vector for both FP64 (left) and FP32 (right) – in the M = 0 sector of the s = 1 icosahedron. Black circles: regular eigenvalues; red circles: ghosts in the Cullum– Willoughby sense.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26261_figures/2605.26261_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Cluster diagnostic for the Krylov-space energy spectrum in the M = 0 sector of the s = 1 icosahedron. The weights 𝑤𝑘 are plotted on a logarithmic scale versus eigenvalues 𝜃𝑘 (in ascending order). Black circles: singletons (not flagged by the ghost diagnostic, or not assigned to a cluster), red circles: ghost-flagged Ritz values, blue circles: total weight of clusters containing ghost-flagged near-duplicate eigenvalues.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a GPU-accelerated version of the Finite-Temperature Lanczos Method designed for workstation hardware. By using a matrix-free approach and optimized indexing, it allows for much larger spin cluster simulations than traditional methods. The study demonstrates that single-precision GPU arithmetic is sufficient for calculating thermodynamic observables accurately.

**Why it may be interesting.** This provides a highly efficient, accessible tool for simulating the thermodynamic properties of large-scale quantum many-body systems using standard workstation hardware.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The exponential growth of memory requirements for storing the Hamiltonian in sparse format prevents large-scale finite-temperature calculations on workstation-grade GPUs.

**Main result.** The authors developed a matrix-free GPU implementation of the Finite-Temperature Lanczos Method (FTLM) that achieves 10x speedups over CPUs and enables Hilbert space dimensions of ~10^8 using single-precision arithmetic without sacrificing physical accuracy.

**Method.** A matrix-free, row-wise gather formulation using two state-to-index strategies: a Compressed Lookup Table (CLT) and a Combinatorial-Ranking (CR) scheme, implemented in MATLAB/CUDA.

**Model / system.** Heisenberg spin Hamiltonians for exchange-coupled spin clusters and magnetic solids, specifically applied to icosahedron and icosidodecahedron geometries with varying spin magnitudes (s = 1/2 to 2).

**Key observables.** Partition function (Z), magnetic susceptibility (chi), heat capacity (C), and magnetization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hilbert space dimension ~10^8, spin magnitude (s), total magnetization sector (M), and single-precision (FP32) vs. double-precision (FP64) arithmetic.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The implementation is optimized for workstation GPUs rather than distributed clusters and currently relies on S_z symmetry.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4 and 5 show the distribution of Ritz eigenvalues and the impact of ghost eigenvalues in FP32 vs FP64; Table 3 compares wall-clock time scaling between CPU and GPU kernels.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational bottleneck of Hamiltonian storage, presents the matrix-free GPU implementation and its indexing strategies (CLT and CR), evaluates numerical precision and error scales, and concludes with performance benchmarks and scaling studies.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a GPU implementation of the finite-temperature Lanczos method (FTLM) for Heisenberg spin Hamiltonians that targets workstation hardware rather than distributed-memory clusters. The Hamiltonian action is evaluated matrix-free in a row-wise gather formulation. We introduce and compare two state-to-index strategies: a compressed lookup table (CLT), which reduces lookup memory by a factor of 16 relative to a full table while retaining a fixed, branch-light access pattern, and a GPU-adapted combinatorial-ranking scheme that removes the lookup table altogether. Numerical tests against FP64 CPU references show that FP32 GPU arithmetic changes heat capacities and magnetic susceptibilities by amounts several orders of magnitude below the stochastic uncertainty of the FTLM trace estimator at typical sample sizes. Benchmarks show speedups of up to about one order of magnitude over optimized multicore CPU calculations and enable Hilbert-space sectors of dimension ~10^8 on a single workstation GPU. The MATLAB/CUDA implementation, including example input files and benchmark scripts, is openly available at https://github.com/ghasdeke/ftlm-gpu (archived at DOI: 10.5281/zenodo.20378647) under the Apache-2.0 license.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27262"></a>
### [Nonasymptotic bounds for quantum purity amplification](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27262v1)

**Authors:** Thilo Scharnhorst, Jack Spilecki, John Wright  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27262v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27262_figures/2605.27262_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 2: Illustration of the overhang bi := µi −λi+1, shown for a fixed SSYT T of shape λ = (6, 3, 1) over the alphabet [3]. Removing the darker gray boxes, which contain the letter d = 3, leaves the sub-tableau T &lt;d</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides new, nonasymptotic bounds for quantum purity amplification that hold for finite numbers of noisy quantum state copies. Unlike previous works that only describe the infinite-sample limit, this result shows how the required number of samples scales with the desired fidelity and the spectral gap of the input state. The derivation uses advanced combinatorial tools like random Young diagrams and the RSK correspondence.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum information processing and open quantum systems, as it provides rigorous, finite-sample guarantees for state purification protocols which are essential for practical quantum error mitigation and state preparation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the problem of quantum purity amplification (QPA), specifically seeking to establish nonasymptotic bounds on the number of noisy state copies required to prepare a high-fidelity target pure state.

**Main result.** The authors establish a nonasymptotic guarantee that n copies suffice to achieve fidelity 1-delta if n scales with k, delta, and the spectral gap between the two largest eigenvalues, independent of the dimension d.

**Method.** The proof utilizes the combinatorics of random Young diagrams, the Robinson-Schensted-Knuth (RSK) correspondence, and the representation theory of the symmetric and unitary groups (Schur-Weyl duality).

**Model / system.** The model involves n copies of a d-dimensional noisy density matrix rho, where the task is to transform these copies into k copies of the principal eigenstate.

**Key observables.** Output fidelity relative to the target state, sample complexity (n), and the spectral gap (p_d - p_{d-1}).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of input copies (n), number of output copies (k), error parameter (delta), and the eigenvalues of the input state (p_i).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The principal eigenstate is assumed to be unique (p_{d-1} < p_d), and the bounds are derived for arbitrary spectra.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 illustrates the concept of 'overhang' in a Young diagram by showing how removing boxes labeled d affects the diagram structure.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QPA problem and the gap in existing asymptotic literature, defines the QPA channel using representation theory, develops a combinatorial fidelity formula using RSK correspondence, and concludes with the derivation of the nonasymptotic complexity bound.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In quantum purity amplification, one is given $n$ copies of a noisy quantum state $ρ\in \mathbb{C}^{d \times d}$ and asked to prepare $k$ copies of its principal eigenstate $|v_d\rangle$. Several prior works have derived information-theoretically optimal algorithms for this problem, but the bounds they prove are only shown in the asymptotic regime as the number of samples $n$ tends to infinity. In this paper, we establish the following nonasymptotic guarantee: if $ρ$'s eigenvalues are sorted $p_1 \leq \cdots \leq p_d$ and $p_{d-1} < p_d$, then \begin{equation*}   n = O\Big(k + \frac{k}δ \cdot \frac{1-p_d}{(p_d-p_{d-1})^2}\Big)   \end{equation*} copies suffice to output a state with fidelity at least $1-δ$ with $|v_d^{\otimes k}\rangle$. Our bound holds for arbitrary spectra, and is independent of the dimension $d$. In the case of depolarizing noise, our finite-sample guarantee matches the optimal asymptotic scaling. Our proof is based on the combinatorics of random Young diagrams.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27278"></a>
### [Optimal quantum locally differentially private mechanisms in the high-privacy regime](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27278v1)

**Authors:** Yuuya Yoshida  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27278v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27278_figures/2605.27278_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Graphs of S1(⃗σ∗)/S1 C(n, ε) and A1(⃗σ∗)/A1 C(n, ε). The hori- zontal axes represent the values of ε, and the vertical axes represent the values of the two ratios. The black dotted lines, the blue dashed lines, and the red solid lines are the cases n = 3, n = 6, and n = 10, respec- tively.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27278_figures/2605.27278_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Graphs of S1(⃗σ)/S1 C(10, ε) and A1(⃗σ)/A1 C(10, ε). The hori- zontal axes represent the values of ε, and the vertical axes represent the values of the two ratios. The red solid lines and the red dotted lines are the cases ⃗σ = ⃗σ∗and ⃗σ = ⃗σ∗∗, respectively.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper identifies a fundamental quantum advantage in the high-privacy regime of local differential privacy. It proves that for datasets with three or more items, quantum mechanisms provide a strictly higher utility-to-privacy ratio than classical mechanisms, regardless of the specific information-theoretic metric used.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the trade-off between privacy and utility in the high-privacy regime (epsilon -> 0) for Quantum Local Differential Privacy (QLDP) compared to classical LDP.

**Main result.** The paper proves that in the high-privacy regime, the ratio of quantum to classical optimal utility (Q/C) is constant regardless of the utility function and demonstrates a quantum advantage of at least 3/2 for data with cardinality n >= 3.

**Method.** The author uses second-order Taylor expansions, Fréchet derivatives, and Petz monotone metrics to analyze utility functions, alongside frame theory (isoclinic mechanisms) to construct optimal quantum mechanisms.

**Model / system.** The framework involves a data collector estimating information from private data provided via quantum states (QLDP) or classical probability distributions (LDP), using density matrices on a d-dimensional Hilbert space.

**Key observables.** Holevo information, mutual information, error exponents in symmetric and asymmetric hypothesis testing, and f-divergences.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Privacy parameter epsilon (specifically the limit epsilon -> 0), data cardinality n, and the dimension d of the Hilbert space.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The utility functions are assumed to be symmetric, unitary invariant, and possess a positive second derivative (Hessian) at the uniform distribution; the quantum advantage disappears for binary data (n=2).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 plots the ratios of symmetric and asymmetric error exponents against epsilon for different n, illustrating the presence of quantum advantage for small epsilon.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the privacy-utility trade-off, establishes the mathematical framework for LDP and QLDP, proves the fundamental asymptotic ratio theorem, constructs optimal isoclinic mechanisms, and analyzes specific hypothesis testing scenarios.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We optimize the trade-off between privacy and utility in the high-privacy regime. We adopt local differential privacy (LDP) and its quantum extension, quantum local differential privacy (QLDP), for privacy protection, and investigate utility functions including the Holevo information (which reduces to the mutual information in the classical case) and the error exponents in symmetric and asymmetric hypothesis testing. These utility functions have classical and quantum optimal values, which are denoted by $C$ and $Q$, respectively, in this abstract for simplicity. In this paper, we provide optimal LDP and QLDP mechanisms achieving the classical and quantum optimal values in the high-privacy regime, and prove that the asymptotic ratio $Q/C$ in this regime takes the same value regardless of the utility function. Our results reveal quantum advantages (more precisely, $Q/C\ge3/2$) for the above utility functions when the protected private data are $n$-ary with $n\ge3$.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.26214"></a>
### [Trade-off Relation for Black Hole Entropy Fluctuations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26214v1)

**Authors:** Kensuke Gallock-Yoshimura, Yoshihiko Hasegawa  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26214v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26214_figures/2605.26214_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The Penrose diagram of a black hole with stationary trajectories of the superposed charge and a schematic picture of the DSW experiment. No radiation will be propagated to I + so long as the experiment is performed adiabatically. Here, V1 and V2 are two “times” on the horizon H, and C is a cross section of the black hole.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a fundamental limit on the precision with which a black hole can record quantum information, showing that entropy fluctuations are unavoidable. It proves that the relative variance of the entropy change is inversely proportional to the number of infalling photons. This result links the stochastic nature of semiclassical gravity to the information-carrying capacity of quantum fields.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether black hole horizons can record quantum information with arbitrarily small entropy fluctuations and seeks to establish a fundamental trade-off relation between entropy variance and the amount of infalling quantum information.

**Main result.** The authors derive a fundamental trade-off relation where the relative variance of the black hole entropy change is bounded from below by the inverse of the photon number: Var[Delta delta S] / (E[Delta delta S])^2 >= 1 / (4 <N>).

**Method.** The study utilizes stochastic semiclassical gravity and the Einstein-Langevin equations, employing the Magnus expansion, the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality, and the gravitational phase space method to analyze fluctuations in the energy-momentum tensor.

**Model / system.** The system consists of an asymptotically flat, stationary Schwarzschild black hole interacting with infalling quantum matter fields (photons) within the framework of the Danielson-Satishchandran-Wald decoherence mechanism.

**Key observables.** Stochastic variance of black hole entropy change (Var[Delta delta S]), expectation value of entropy change (E[Delta delta S]), photon number (<N>), and quantum mutual information (I).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Planck length (l_P), Schwarzschild radius (r_h), effective dipole moment (ql), and the number of entangling photons.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The field state is assumed to be a quasifree Hadamard state, and the spacetime is assumed to be stationary and asymptotically flat.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a Penrose diagram of a black hole and a schematic of the DSW experiment, illustrating a superposed charge and the capture of entangling photons by the horizon.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the physical problem and the DSW decoherence mechanism, proceeds to establish the mathematical framework of stochastic semiclassical gravity, derives the fundamental trade-off relations and uncertainty bounds using field-theoretic methods, and concludes with implications for horizon area fluctuations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Black holes respond to infalling quantum matter fields by changing their entropy. Since such matter is quantum in nature, the entropy response should be sensitive to its quantum fluctuations. We show, within stochastic semiclassical gravity, that a horizon cannot record relevant quantum information with arbitrarily small entropy fluctuations. For the infalling photons encoding which-path information in the Danielson-Satishchandran-Wald decoherence experiment, we derive a trade-off relation between the stochastic variance of the black hole entropy change and the photon number.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.26331"></a>
### [Uncertainty Relation for a Single Observable](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26331v1)

**Authors:** Haruki Yamashita, Aina Mayumi, Gen Kimura  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26331v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26331_figures/2605.26331_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. State purity versus the averaged lower bounds ob- tained from the Robertson relation (34), the Schr¨odinger re- lation (35), the Luo-type relation (31), and the optimal rela- tions (32) and the sharper relation (33) derived in this paper.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper establishes that the uncertainty of a single quantum observable has an intrinsic lower bound determined by its noncommutativity with the quantum state. It introduces a method to decompose variance into classical and quantum components, achieving an exact identity for qubit systems. These new relations are shown to be tighter than traditional uncertainty bounds like the Robertson or Schrodinger relations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum information and open quantum systems as it provides a fundamental way to quantify the 'quantumness' or coherence of an observable relative to a state, which is crucial for understanding decoherence and resource theories.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the uncertainty of a single observable admits a non-trivial lower bound based on the noncommutativity between the observable and the quantum state.

**Main result.** The authors derive optimal, state-dependent lower bounds for the variance of a single observable and show that for qubits, these bounds provide an exact decomposition of variance into classical and noncommutative parts.

**Method.** The study uses spectral decomposition, the Hilbert-Schmidt norm, and the pinching map to decompose variance, alongside optimization of coefficients for a family of uncertainty relations.

**Model / system.** The work considers general finite-dimensional quantum systems (Hilbert space C^d) and provides detailed analysis for qubit systems using the Bloch vector representation.

**Key observables.** Variance of a single observable (V_rho(A)), Wigner-Yanase-type quantity, and commutator norms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Eigenvalues of the density operator (lambda_max, lambda_min), purity (P), and the parameter s in the family of relations s in [1/2, infinity).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results exclude the maximally mixed state where the commutator vanishes, and the specific identity results are derived for qubit systems.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares the averaged lower bounds of various uncertainty relations (Robertson, Schrodinger, Luo, and the authors' optimal bound) against the purity of the state, demonstrating the superiority of the proposed bounds.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the single-observable problem, proves optimal lower bounds for general systems, refines these bounds by adding classical uncertainty, provides an exact identity for qubits, and concludes with a comparative analysis of averaged uncertainty products.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Uncertainty relations are usually formulated as trade-off relations between two or more observables. Here we show that the uncertainty of a single observable already has a nontrivial lower bound originating from the noncommutativity between the observable and the quantum state. We prove sharp lower bounds on the variance of a single observable and then sharpen them further by introducing the classical uncertainty of the observable under a fixed state. The optimal coefficient is determined solely by the smallest and largest eigenvalues of the state. Our results include an optimal state-dependent improvement of Luo's Wigner--Yanase-type relation and a direct bound showing that coherence or asymmetry of the state with respect to the observable gives an unavoidable contribution to its uncertainty. For qubits, the sharpened bounds become exact identities, giving a complete decomposition of the variance into classical and noncommutative parts. These single-observable relations also yield improved product-form uncertainty relations for pairs of observables.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.26412"></a>
### [Multi-Scale Coherence of Represented Flows](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26412v1)

**Authors:** Amir Jafari  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26412v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26412_figures/2605.26412_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: Matched second-order statistics for two synthetic divergence-free vector fields. Solid blue denotes Field A and dashed orange denotes Field B. The left panel shows the shell-averaged spectrum E(k), and the right panel shows the normalized scalar two-point correlation C(r)/C(0). The two fields are indistinguishable at this second-order level, up to numerical roundoff.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26412_figures/2605.26412_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2: Cross-resolution coherence for the same two divergence-free fields as in Fig. 1. The left heatmap shows Field A and the right heatmap shows Field B. The plotted quantity is the signed coherence SℓL, averaged over space, separation directions, and sampled separations r, then binned by the resolution ratios L/r and ℓ/r. Although the two fields have identical Fourier amplitudes, matched spectra, and matched scalar two-point correlations, their coherence matrices differ, showing that second-order statistics do not determine cross-resolution directional organization.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26412_figures/2605.26412_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3: Representation dependence of cross-resolution coherence for the Lorenz vector field. The left heatmap shows the signed coherence matrix SℓL in the original phase-space coordinates, while the middle heatmap shows the same diagnostic after a smooth wrinkling of the coordinate chart. The right panel summarizes the same data by averaging SℓL at fixed resolution-ratio gap |L/r −ℓ/r|; solid blue denotes the original coordinates and dashed orange denotes the wrinkled coordinates. The underlying dynamics is unchanged, but the represented finite-separation geometry is modified.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26412_figures/2605.26412_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4: Model mismatch in a sampled Lorenz phase-space vector field. The left panel compares the local stretching proxy σmax(Df) for the Lorenz vector field with the corresponding value for a smooth misspecified model. The middle panel shows the true self-coherence</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26412_figures/2605.26412_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5: FRG truncation geometry in the three-dimensional O(1) LPA flow. From left to right, the heatmaps show the projected self-coherence matrices for M = 4, M = 5, and M = 6 polynomial truncations, followed by the projected M = 4–M = 6 cross-coherence matrix. The plotted quantity is the signed increment coherence SℓL, averaged over sampled theory-space points, displacement directions, and displacement lengths, and binned by L/η and ℓ/η. The self-coherence matrices remain high and structurally similar, while the cross-truncation matrix is systematically reduced.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new diagnostic called the coherence matrix to detect how the geometry of vector fields changes across different scales. It proves that this method can identify structural differences in flows that standard tools like power spectra or Lyapunov exponents fail to detect, specifically in the context of dynamical systems and renormalization-group flows.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the lack of a diagnostic tool capable of testing whether the geometric structure of vector-field increments at finite separations remains stable across different observational resolutions, scales, or representations.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a new 'multi-scale coherence' diagnostic can distinguish between flows that appear identical under standard second-order statistics (spectra and correlations) and can detect geometric mismatches in renormalization-group truncations.

**Method.** A new representation-dependent diagnostic, the coherence matrix, is introduced to compare the direction of vector-field increments after smoothing the field at two different resolutions.

**Model / system.** The method is tested on three distinct systems: synthetic 2D divergence-free vector fields, the Lorenz dynamical system, and the functional renormalization-group (FRG) beta-function flows for a 3D O(1) scalar theory.

**Key observables.** Coherence matrix (S_l_L), power spectra, scalar two-point correlations, local stretching proxies (maximum singular value of the Jacobian), and pointwise beta-vector cosine.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Smoothing resolutions (l, L), separation distance (r), resolution ratios (a = L/r, b = l/r), and the FRG width factor (chi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The diagnostic is representation-dependent and not coordinate-invariant; it depends on the chosen variables, metric, and coarse-graining protocol.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 and 2 show that synthetic fields with identical spectra have distinct coherence matrices; Figure 3 shows how coordinate wrinkling reduces Lorenz field coherence; Figure 5 and 6 demonstrate that cross-truncation coherence in FRG flows decreases as higher-order couplings are activated.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the multi-scale coherence diagnostic, then applies it to three test cases (synthetic fields, Lorenz system, and FRG flows) to demonstrate its ability to capture geometric information invisible to standard statistics, before concluding with its utility as a complementary tool.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many problems in nonlinear and statistical physics are formulated through represented flows, including physical-space vector fields, phase-space drift fields, and truncated renormalization-group beta functions. We introduce a complementary representation-dependent diagnostic for testing whether finite-separation flow geometry is stable across observational resolution. For two separated points, states, or theories, the method compares the direction of the corresponding vector-field increment after the field has been smoothed at two resolutions. Averaging this normalized comparison over sampled separations gives a coherence matrix tied to the chosen variables, coarse graining, metric, and sampling protocol; it is a consistency test, not a coordinate-invariant quantity. We demonstrate the diagnostic in three settings. Synthetic divergence-free fields with identical Fourier amplitudes, spectra, and scalar two-point correlations nevertheless produce distinct coherence matrices, showing that second-order statistics do not determine cross-resolution increment geometry. Lorenz phase-space tests show that a smooth coordinate wrinkling changes represented drift geometry without changing the underlying dynamics, and that a weak model perturbation lowers finite-separation coherence even when local stretching proxies remain closely matched. Finally, for functional renormalization-group flows of the three-dimensional \(O(1)\) scalar theory, projected \(M=4,5,6\) LPA beta fields remain internally coherent, while cross-truncation coherence decreases as higher-order coupling directions are activated. The diagnostic provides a practical field-level check of how representations, models, and truncations preserve finite-separation flow geometry, complementing rather than replacing standard local, spectral, or fixed-point diagnostics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.26697"></a>
### [A Gauge-Covariant Theoretical Framework for Non-Abelian Holonomy Estimation and Feed-Forward Correction in Time-Bin Photonic Qudits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26697v1)

**Authors:** N. Josef Bruzzese  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26697v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper develops a new way to calibrate quantum gates that suffer from non-Abelian geometric errors, specifically for photonic qudits. It replaces simple phase subtraction with a matrix-based method that is robust even when the choice of basis changes. This allows for more accurate error correction in complex, high-dimensional quantum computing architectures.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum optics and photonic QI as it provides a rigorous mathematical foundation for calibrating high-dimensional, non-Abelian geometric gates in complex photonic circuits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Estimating and correcting non-Abelian geometric distortions (Wilczek-Zee holonomies) in quantum systems where the encoded object is a transported logical subspace rather than independent rays.

**Main result.** A gauge-covariant theoretical framework for reconstructing non-Abelian holonomy using polar decomposition of overlap matrices, providing robust left- and right-acting feed-forward correction rules.

**Method.** A discrete estimator constructed from the polar decomposition of overlap matrices between successive subspace frames, validated through numerical studies using synthetic non-Abelian transport models.

**Model / system.** Time-bin photonic qudits where a rank-m logical subspace is subject to transport along a path in parameter space, potentially involving mode mixing or effective degeneracies.

**Key observables.** Matrix-valued holonomies, spectrum of the holonomy, Wilson-loop traces, and the minimum singular value of overlap matrices as a conditioning diagnostic.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rank m of the subspace, control parameters lambda, and the conditioning parameter mu_min (minimum singular value).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reconstruction assumes well-conditioned overlap matrices (singular values bounded away from zero) and a smooth (C2) frame path; it does not assume a specific device-specific transfer matrix or loss model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 outlines the reconstruction algorithm; Figure 2 shows convergence of Frobenius error with refinement; Figure 3/6 includes a diagnostic for projector-step refinement.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the failure of Abelian calibration in subspace architectures, proposes a matrix-valued gauge-covariant alternative, provides mathematical proofs for optimality and stability, and validates the framework via numerical simulations of synthetic transport models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop a theoretical and computational framework for estimating and correcting non-Abelian geometric distortions in time-bin photonic qudit processing when the relevant encoded object is a transported logical subspace rather than a collection of independent rays. In such settings, for example under mode mixing, multiplexed routing, or effective degeneracies, the geometric contribution is naturally matrix-valued and is described by a Wilczek-Zee holonomy on a rank-$m$ sub-bundle of the ambient Hilbert space. The framework generalises prior Abelian time-bin Pancharatnam-Berry feed-forward calibration, in which geometric distortions are represented by bin-resolved scalar phases, to the non-Abelian, matrix-valued case. We construct a gauge-covariant discrete estimator from overlap matrices between successive subspace frames: the polar factor of each overlap gives a unitary backward frame comparator, and the adjoint comparators compose to approximate the forward path-ordered exponential of the Wilczek-Zee connection. We prove gauge covariance under frame changes, polar optimality of the local comparator, consistency under partition refinement, and perturbative stability under well-conditioned overlap errors. We then formulate left- and right-acting feed-forward correction rules for removing the estimated holonomy from an effective logical operation. The work does not assume a device-specific transfer matrix, loss model, detector model, or experimental calibration pipeline; numerical studies use synthetic non-Abelian transport models to validate covariance, convergence, conditioning dependence, and correction fidelity.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.27193"></a>
### [Algebras of order parameters in one-dimensional spin systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27193v1)

**Authors:** Ameya Chavda, Clement Delcamp, Alex Turzillo, Minyoung You  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27193v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27193_figures/2605.27193_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a unified algebraic framework to classify string order parameters in 1D systems with non-invertible symmetries. It demonstrates that these order parameters form a Lagrangian algebra in the Drinfeld center of the symmetry category. The results extend the understanding of phase transitions and symmetry breaking to both pure and mixed-state quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and open quantum systems as it extends the classification of order parameters to symmetric mixed states and provides a rigorous algebraic framework for non-invertible symmetries.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the algebraic properties of string order parameters in 1D quantum lattice models with both invertible and non-invertible symmetries, specifically focusing on the role of the multiplication rule in the Lagrangian algebra.

**Main result.** The authors prove that multiplets of string order parameters organize into a Lagrangian algebra within the Drinfeld center of the symmetry category and that the expectation value of a string operator depends on the associated tube algebra module.

**Method.** The study utilizes tensor network approaches, specifically block-injective Matrix Product States (MPS), combined with the mathematical framework of fusion category theory, module categories, and the SymTFT construction.

**Model / system.** One-dimensional quantum lattice models (spin systems) characterized by finite invertible or non-invertible (fusion) symmetries, including both gapped phases and symmetric mixed states.

**Key observables.** String order parameters, twisted sector local operators, and expectation values of non-local operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gapped phases, spontaneous symmetry breaking (SSB) regimes, infrared (IR) limit, and non-anomalous symmetry categories.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The symmetries are assumed to be finite and semi-simple (fusion categories), and the gapped phases are represented by block-injective MPS.

**Paper structure.** The paper moves from the classical Landau paradigm to SPT phases and non-invertible symmetries, establishing a unified algebraic description through tensor networks, classifying gapped phases via module categories, and providing explicit character and Omega-symbol derivations for the Drinfeld center.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study order parameters in one-dimensional quantum lattice models with finite invertible or non-invertible symmetry. We investigate what properties a string operator must satisfy in order to acquire a non-vanishing expectation value in a given gapped phase. We deduce that multiplets of string order parameters organise into a Lagrangian algebra in the Drinfel'd centre of the symmetry category. In particular, we highlight the role of the multiplication rule as governing the fusion of the twisted sector local operators that constitute the string operator in the infrared limit. Our derivations exploit the tensor network approach to the classification of gapped phases and its reformulation in terms of module categories over the symmetry category. Within this framework, a gapped phase is associated with a pattern of spontaneous symmetry breaking wherein a Morita class of algebras of topological lines is preserved in the ground state subspace. The crux of the proof is to show that the expectation value of any string operator explicitly depends on the tube algebra module associated with the Lagrangian algebra, which is realised as the full centre of the corresponding module category. Finally, we demonstrate that these techniques extend to phases of symmetric mixed states.

</details>

**Authors:** Zongbo, Bao, Jop Briët, Davi Castro-Silva, Philippe van Dordrecht, Jonas Helsen

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a way to efficiently test if a quantum gate belongs to the third level of the Clifford hierarchy. It achieves this by proving that if a specific mathematical norm is large, the gate must be close to the desired hierarchy level, bridging quantum information with additive combinatorics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the problem of testing whether an unknown unitary belongs to a specific level of the Clifford hierarchy and seeks to establish inverse theorems for noncommutative Pauli uniformity norms.

**Main result.** The authors establish a 99% inverse theorem for the fourth Pauli uniformity norm and provide an efficient algorithm (C3Tester) to distinguish unitaries close to the third level of the Clifford hierarchy.

**Method.** The study uses higher-order Fourier analysis, inductive arguments adapted from additive combinatorics, and the construction of unitary representations of the Pauli group via Pauli derivatives.

**Model / system.** The mathematical framework involves unitary operators acting on a finite-dimensional Hilbert space (n-qudits) and the algebraic structure of the Pauli group and Clifford hierarchy.

**Key observables.** Degree-k Clifford fidelity and the k-th Pauli uniformity norm (Pk).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The 99% regime (near-extremizers), the 1% regime, and the hierarchy level k.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the 99% setting where the norm is close to its maximum; the extension to higher levels (k >= 5) is limited by the lack of multiplicative closure in the hierarchy.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the testing problem, defines the Pauli uniformity norms, presents the construction of the C3Tester algorithm, proves the 99% inverse theorem for k up to 4 using induction, and discusses the barriers to higher-order extensions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the problem of testing whether an unknown unitary is close to a specified level of the Clifford hierarchy. Bu, Gu, and Jaffe proposed a candidate tester for this task based on a connection with noncommutative analogues of the Gowers uniformity norms. The complexity of this tester -- whose analysis depends on a robust characterization of the near-extremizers of these norms -- was left open. We establish such a characterization for the fourth noncommutative uniformity norm and, as a consequence, obtain an efficient tester for the third level of the Clifford hierarchy. We further discuss possible routes toward resolving the problem of testing for all higher levels, highlighting the main barriers that remain.

</details>

### [Probing Spacetime Topology and Superposition with Accelerated Detectors](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26490v1)

**Authors:** P. Poopathysankar, Lucas Hackl, Anwesha Chakraborty  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26490v1>

**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores how the geometric properties of spacetime, such as periodic dimensions and quantum superpositions of geometries, influence the ability of accelerated probes to harvest entanglement from a vacuum field. It demonstrates that topological features can protect entanglement against the noise induced by the Unruh effect. The findings suggest that entanglement harvesting could serve as an operational tool to detect quantum features of spacetime.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the interplay of relativistic acceleration, spacetime topology (compactification), and quantum superposition of geometries affects the extraction of entanglement from a quantum field.

**Main result.** Compactification and spacetime superposition both expand the parameter space for entanglement harvesting, particularly in high-acceleration regimes, while anti-parallel acceleration yields higher entanglement than parallel acceleration.

**Method.** The authors use the Unruh-DeWitt entanglement harvesting protocol within a perturbative framework, calculating Wightman functions in quotient Rindler spacetimes and their superpositions.

**Model / system.** Two-level Unruh-DeWitt detectors following Rindler trajectories (parallel and anti-parallel) interacting with a massless scalar field in both compactified and superposed Minkowski/Rindler geometries.

**Key observables.** Concurrence, Negativity, local excitation probability (noise), and cross-correlation terms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Acceleration (a), detector separation (R), compactification length (L), detector energy gap (Omega), and switching function width (sigma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Weak coupling limit, Gaussian switching function, massless scalar field, and untwisted field boundary conditions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows detector trajectories; Figure 2 plots concurrence across different spacetime configurations; Figure 3 presents a phase diagram of the entanglement harvesting region; Figure 4 shows concurrence as a function of compactification length and acceleration.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of entanglement harvesting in complex geometries, defines the mathematical model of detectors and spacetime, presents numerical results for various acceleration and topology configurations, and concludes with the physical implications for probing spacetime features.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study entanglement harvested by Unruh DeWitt detectors following Rindler trajectories in compactified and superposed Minkowski spacetime. We consider different directions of acceleration (both parallel and antiparallel), separation between detectors and direction of spatial compactification mutually perpendicular to each other. Using the standard entanglement harvesting protocol, we analyze how these features influence the extracted correlations. When detector separation is perpendicular to the direction of acceleration, the harvested entanglement is uniformly suppressed due to increased spacelike separation. Compactification enhances field correlations leading to an increased concurrence and an extended harvesting range at higher accelerations. Additionally, we show that spacetime superposition introduces interference effects that further enlarge the entanglement harvesting region in parameter space, particularly in the high acceleration regime. We also find that the effect of antiparallel acceleration yielding significantly higher entanglement than parallel acceleration prevails in compactified and superposed spacetime.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.27145"></a>
### [Subdiffusion equation with Cattaneo effect](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27145v1)

**Authors:** Tadeusz Kosztołowicz, Aldona Dutkiewicz, Katarzyna D. Lewandowska  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27145v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27145_figures/2605.27145_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Plots of Green’s functions for short times given in the legend. Lines with filled symbols represent Green’s functions Eq. (30) for the subdiffusion equation with the Cattaneo effect, for 𝛼= 0.5, 𝜅= 0.9, 𝜏= 10, and 𝐷= 10. Lines with empty symbols are GF Eq. (34) plots for the subdiffusion equation without this effect, for 𝛼= 0.5 and 𝐷= 10.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27145_figures/2605.27145_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Relative changes of the GF 𝑃𝑅Eq. (35) for the functions presented in Fig. 1, for parameters given in the legend.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27145_figures/2605.27145_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 5: Time evolution of MSD for 𝛼= 0.5 and 𝜅= 0.9. The line with filled symbols is the plot of 𝜎2 𝑆𝑇(𝑡) for short times Eq. (39), the line with open symbols represents 𝜎2 𝐿𝑇(𝑡) for long times Eq. (40). The calculations have been made taking into account the 50 leading terms in the series defining the functions. The boundary between short and long times is at the common point of both lines at 𝑡≈220. The line without symbols represents the time evolution of the MSD for the equation without the Cattaneo effect Eq. (37).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27145_figures/2605.27145_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 6: Plots of the time evolution of MSD for 𝛼= 0.9 and 𝜅= 0.5, the description is as for Fig. 5.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a modified subdiffusion equation that incorporates a Cattaneo-type delay to prevent the non-physical infinite propagation velocity found in standard fractional diffusion models. It shows that while the effect significantly alters short-time dynamics, the system remains subdiffusive in the long-time limit. The work also emphasizes the necessity of including this delay effect in boundary conditions for physical consistency.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the non-physical infinite propagation velocity in ordinary subdiffusion equations by introducing a Cattaneo-type effect that incorporates a random time delay in flux activation.

**Main result.** The authors derive a Cattaneo-type subdiffusion equation (CTSE) that ensures a finite propagation velocity and demonstrate that the Cattaneo effect modifies short-time dynamics without inducing true superdiffusion.

**Method.** The study uses Laplace transforms, Riemann-Liouville and Caputo fractional derivatives, and series expansions to solve the fractional differential equations and analyze asymptotic limits.

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional homogeneous system describing subdiffusive processes (such as transport in viscoelastic media or porous media) where the flux is regulated by a Mittag-Leffler-controlled delay distribution.

**Key observables.** Green's function (probability density), Mean Square Displacement (MSD), and the probability density of particle absorption at a partially absorbing wall.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Subdiffusion exponent (alpha), Cattaneo effect parameter (kappa), delay strength (tau), diffusion coefficient (D), and wall absorption parameter (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The diffusion parameters are assumed to be time-independent and independent of concentration; the Cattaneo effect is assumed to be present in both the governing equation and the boundary conditions for physical consistency.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare Green's functions and MSD evolution with and without the Cattaneo effect across short and long time limits, and illustrate the impact of including the effect in boundary conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of infinite velocity, derives the CTSE using Laplace transforms, analyzes the short-time and long-time asymptotic behaviors of the Green's function and MSD, investigates boundary conditions at a partially absorbing wall, and proposes an experimental identification method.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The ordinary subdiffusion equation, with a fractional time derivative of at most first order, describes a process in which the propagation velocity of diffusing molecules is unlimited. To avoid this non-physical property different forms of the Cattaneo subdiffusion equation have been proposed. We define the Cattaneo effect as a delay of the ordinary subdiffusion flux activation by a random time. By incorporating this effect into the flux equation we get a Cattaneo--type subdiffusion equation (CTSE). We consider a subdiffusion process in which the Cattaneo effect is generated by the time-delay probability distribution controlled by the Mittag-Leffler function. Then, CTSE differs from the ordinary subdiffusion equation by a term with a fractional time derivative, whose order can be independent of the subdiffusion exponent. The influence of the Cattaneo effect on the solutions to the CTSE is discussed. We show that the process described by CTSE is subdiffusion in the entire time domain even though the temporal evolution of the mean square displacement of diffusing particle in the short-time limit is typical for superdiffusion. The delay in the flux activation in the subdiffusion equation should also cause a flux delay in a boundary condition. As an example, we study subdiffusion with the Cattaneo effect in a system with a partially absorbing wall at which the Robin boundary condition is assumed. We also propose a method for experimentally identifying the Cattaneo effect in a subdiffusive system.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.26224"></a>
### [$S$-duality, boundary states, and higher-form symmetries on ALE spaces](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26224v1)

**Authors:** Mohamed M. Anber  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26224v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26224_figures/2605.26224_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Schematic illustration of an ALE space of A3-type, equivalently the resolution of the orbifold singularity C2/Z4. There is a chain of three 2-spheres Σ1, Σ2, Σ3, whose intersections reproduce the A3 Dynkin diagram. The asymptotic boundary is the lens space S3/Z4. In the convention shown, the intersection pairing is written as the positive Cartan matrix, with Σi · Σi = 2 and Σi · Σi±1 = −1.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26224_figures/2605.26224_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Decomposition of the weight lattice P of A2 into cosets of the root lattice Q. The lattice points split into three disjoint families, Q + wµ with µ = 0, 1, 2, corresponding to the three elements of P/Q ∼= Z3. The blue circles, red squares, and green triangles represent the three cosets Q + w0 = Q, Q + w1, and Q + w2, respectively. Each coset defines a sector function Θµ(τ) = P λ∈Q+wµ eiπτλ2. Here w0 = 0, while α1 and α2 denote the simple roots generating Q.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26224_figures/2605.26224_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3. A path integral on a manifold M with boundary ∂M prepares a state |Ψ⟩in the Hilbert space H∂M associated with the boundary.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26224_figures/2605.26224_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4. Gluing the Eguchi–Hanson space XEH to its orientation reversal XEH along the common lens-space boundary S3/Z2. The resulting closed 4-manifold is diffeomorphic to S2 × S2.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper resolves the apparent breakdown of S-duality on non-compact ALE spaces by treating the path integral as a vector-valued boundary state. It demonstrates that these 'chiral building blocks' can be glued together to recover standard modularly covariant partition functions on closed manifolds. The work also explores the role of 1-form symmetries and the associated mixed electric-magnetic anomalies.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the apparent failure of S-duality (modularity) for the Maxwell path integral on non-compact A-type ALE spaces and determining how higher-form symmetries and boundary states resolve this.

**Main result.** The path integral on ALE spaces is reinterpreted as a vector-valued boundary state whose components (theta-function blocks) transform via vector-valued modular covariance, successfully recovering the standard partition function on closed manifolds like S2 x S2 upon gluing.

**Method.** The author uses the Gibbons-Hawking ansatz for field construction, evaluates the Maxwell path integral, and employs a gluing construction (pairing boundary states) to validate the results on closed manifolds.

**Model / system.** Abelian Maxwell theory on A-type Asymptely Locally Euclidean (ALE) spaces, which are hyperkähler manifolds approaching R4/Z_N at infinity, with an asymptotic boundary of a lens space S3/Z_N.

**Key observables.** Complexified coupling (tau), theta-function blocks (Theta_mu), partition functions, 1-form symmetry backgrounds, and Wilson/t'Hooft line operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Complexified coupling tau = theta/2pi + 4pi*i/g^2, N-ality (mu), and the order of discrete Z_k subgroups.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses specifically on A-type ALE spaces and assumes the existence of well-defined boundary conditions (e.g., Dirichlet) for the gauge fields.

**Figures summary.** A schematic illustration of an A3-type ALE space showing a chain of three 2-spheres.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by identifying the modularity failure on ALE spaces, introduces the boundary state formalism and vector-valued modular covariance, validates the construction via gluing Eguchi-Hanson spaces to form S2 x S2, and then extends the analysis to include 1-form symmetry backgrounds and discrete gauging.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study Abelian $S$-duality of Maxwell theory on $A$-type asymptotically locally Euclidean (ALE) spaces. Unlike on closed four-manifolds, the Maxwell path integral on an ALE space is not naturally a scalar partition function. Rather, it decomposes into theta-function blocks labeled by flat $U(1)$ holonomy sectors on the asymptotic lens-space boundary. We interpret these blocks as components of the Hilbert-space boundary state prepared by the ALE path integral. With this interpretation, the apparent failure of ordinary modularity is replaced by vector-valued modular covariance under the action of the modular group. We test this picture explicitly for Eguchi-Hanson space by gluing it to its orientation reversal. The resulting closed four-manifold is diffeomorphic to $S^2\times S^2$, and the natural pairing of the two ALE boundary states reproduces the standard Maxwell partition function on $S^2\times S^2$. We then refine the construction by turning on electric and magnetic $1$-form symmetry backgrounds. In their presence, the ALE theta blocks are not ordinary functions, but sections of a line bundle over the Cartan torus associated with the $A_{N-1}$ root lattice, reflecting the mixed electric-magnetic $1$-form anomaly. We also discuss gauging discrete $\mathbb Z_k$ subgroups of the $1$-form symmetries and show that the vector-valued boundary-state structure remains the natural covariant framework after gauging. In this sense, ALE spaces behave as chiral building blocks for four-dimensional Maxwell theory: individual ALE blocks carry sector-resolved boundary data, while gluing pairs these sectors to produce an ordinary closed-manifold partition function, much like the pairing of left- and right-moving conformal blocks in two-dimensional CFT.

</details>

### [A Resource Comparison of Logical T-State Preparation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26522v1)

**Authors:** Jianshuo Gao, Xiao Yuan, Yuan Yao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26522v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26522_figures/2605.26522_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Logical-level magic-state-injection gadget for implementing a logical T gate. The data block controls a logical CNOT into a prepared logical magic-state, the magic-state block is measured in the logical Z basis, and the measurement result m ∈{0, 1} controls the Clifford correction Sm L . With this convention, the output is TL |ψL⟩up to an irrelevant global phase [7, 8].</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26522_figures/2605.26522_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Representative logical |T⟩-preparation routes. (a) Distillation processes several noisy logical magic-states using a Clifford check circuit, measures stabilizer checks to obtain a syndrome vector s, and keeps the run only when s = s⋆; otherwise the run is discarded [8, 11, 15]. (b) Cultivation injects a seed into a distance-3 color code, grows and verifies it, and grafts accepted states into a larger surface-code patch [23, 24]. (c) Code-switching prepares a logical |T⟩in the [[15, 1, 3]] qRM code via transversal T, transfers it to the [[7, 1, 3]] Steane code using transversal CNOT, and postselects on the qRM measurement outcomes [19, 20, 22].</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26522_figures/2605.26522_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Expected cost Vexp versus reported output error ϵout for the included data rows. Marker shape indicates paper provenance, and filled markers denote within-family Pareto-frontier points in the displayed plane. Because the native cost unit differs across original papers, the horizontal axis should be read as a regime map rather than as a universal cross-paper ruler.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26522_figures/2605.26522_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: Family-level summary over V , Qpeak, D, ϵout, reporting completeness, and Vexp. Each spoke reports the median normalized value over rows that provide the corresponding field; lower raw values are better for all quantitative resource/error fields.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a systematic comparison of the three primary methods for preparing logical T-states in fault-tolerant quantum computing. It reveals that while code-switching and cultivation are more efficient for low-overhead tasks, only magic state distillation can currently meet the extreme fidelity requirements needed for large-scale algorithms like Shor's algorithm.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified framework to compare the resource overheads of different logical T-state preparation protocols due to heterogeneous reporting conventions in existing literature.

**Main result.** The study identifies a trade-off landscape where distillation is the only viable route for ultra-high-fidelity requirements (e.g., RSA-2048), while code-switching and cultivation offer much lower overhead for lower-fidelity or intermediate-fidelity regimes.

**Method.** A meta-analysis and comparative study of existing quantitative results, using a structured framework that retains source-native cost units and a logarithmic normalization for family-level visualization.

**Model / system.** Fault-tolerant quantum computing architectures based on stabilizer codes, including surface codes, color codes, and qRM codes, specifically focusing on the implementation of non-Clifford T-gates.

**Key observables.** Output error (epsilon_out), single-attempt cost (V), expected cost per accepted output (V_exp), peak qubit footprint (Q_peak), and latency (D).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Error regimes ranging from 1e-6 to 1e-20, RSA-2048 error budgets, and T-state consumption rates (c_T = 4 or 7).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The comparison is bounded by the noise models, decoders, and reporting completeness of the underlying source papers; it does not include concatenated coding.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a T-gate injection gadget; Figure 2 schematically summarizes distillation, cultivation, and code-switching; Figure 3 is a regime map of expected cost vs. output error; Figure 4 is a radar plot of normalized protocol metrics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of heterogeneous comparison, defines a multi-metric framework, presents a comparative analysis of three protocol families (distillation, cultivation, code-switching), provides an algorithm-level case study using Shor's algorithm, and concludes with a summary of the trade-off landscape.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Logical T state preparation is a major overhead source in fault tolerant architectures built from stabilizer operations. Existing protocols, however, are reported under different code families, noise models, postselection rules, and cost conventions, making direct comparison difficult. We compare three representative preparation routes: magic state distillation, magic state cultivation, and code switching, using currently available results. Rather than reducing heterogeneous data to a single cost metric, we retain source native cost units and record output error, single attempt cost, expected cost per accepted output, footprint, latency, and reporting completeness for each configuration. Within the current dataset, distillation reaches the lowest output error regime; code switching achieves the lowest reported single attempt cost and the smallest explicit footprint among the compatible rows; and recent RP2 cultivation results add low cost cultivation points with output errors between 1e-6 and 1e-9. As a simple algorithm level case study, we also examine the reported preparation routes under an error budget motivated by Shor factoring algorithm, in order to relate single state preparation costs to full workload requirements. The resulting comparison clarifies the trade offs currently supported across the literature, while remaining bounded by the conventions and coverage of the underlying papers.

</details>

### [Adaptive Shot Allocation for Recursive QAOA via Reinforcement Learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26544v1)

**Authors:** Euimin Lee, Shiho Kim

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1 Overview of the proposed shot-control framework for depth-1 RQAOA. (A) At each elimina- tion step t, a depth-1 QAOA circuit is executed on the current reduced graph Gt using kt measurement shots to estimate the pairwise correlation matrix M (t) uv . The edge (u∗, v∗) with the largest absolute correlation is selected, and the corresponding variable is eliminated to produce the next reduced graph Gt+1. This procedure repeats until the problem size falls below a classical-solver threshold nc, at which point the residual instance is solved exactly. (B) The RL shot-control loop operates along- side the RQAOA pipeline. Before each elimination step, a small probe measurement of kprobe shots...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26544_figures/2605.26544_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2 Shot reduction (%) vs. problem size n for the held-out operational subset. Each point rep- resents one policy–instance pair. The per-size mean (connected line) shows that RL achieves stable ∼36% reduction across the full range, while the Heuristic settles around ∼23%.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26544_figures/2605.26544_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3 plots normalized shot usage against success rate for all pairs in the held-out operational subset. Relative to Uniform, the RL points are shifted toward lower shot usage while remaining in a high-success regime in the large majority of cases. The Heuristic occupies an intermediate position, with smaller shot savings and slightly higher success rates on average. The RL policy is trained with a constraint target p∗= 0.95, yet the observed success rate on the held-out operational subset is only about 0.91. This gap is not contradictory: the constraint is enforced during training on a designated base instance through the adaptive multiplier λ, whereas test-time evaluation aggregates...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26544_figures/2605.26544_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
