**Summary.** This paper identifies SNR and sample size as the primary controllers of how similar different neural networks become during training. It reveals that the similarity of internal features can be decoupled from how well the network performs its task, specifically showing a dip in alignment at the interpolation threshold.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the fundamental drivers of representational alignment in neural networks and determining how data quality (SNR) and quantity (sample size) influence the similarity of learned internal representations.

**Main result.** The study finds that representational alignment increases monotonically with SNR but varies non-monultically with training sample size, reaching a minimum near the interpolation threshold. Crucially, it demonstrates that alignment and generalization performance are decoupled.

**Method.** The authors use an analytical approach with two-layer linear networks to derive closed-form expressions for alignment and introduce Conditional Copula Entropy (CCE) as a novel, rank-based information-theoretic metric.

**Model / system.** The primary model is a two-layer linear network (single hidden layer) trained via gradient flow on MSE, supplemented by nonlinear ReLU networks and real-world CNNs (CIFAR-10, MNIST) to test universality.

**Key observables.** Conditional Copula Entropy (CCE), generalization error, asymptotic correlation (rho), and Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR), training sample size to dimension ratio (alpha), interpolation threshold (alpha=1), and the underparameterized vs. overparameterized regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analytical derivation assumes a high-dimensional limit (n, d -> infinity), small random initializations (rich learning regime), and that large distances in representations are not informative.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows generalization error and CCE vs. alpha for different SNRs; Figure 2 shows ReLU network performance; Figure 3 compares Linear vs. ReLU informativeness; Figure 4 shows CNN performance on CIFAR-10 under label noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of representational alignment, presents a new metric (CCE), provides analytical derivations for linear networks using spectral theory (Marchenko-Pastur), validates these with nonlinear and real-world experiments, and concludes by discussing the decoupling of alignment and generalization.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Neural networks are known to develop latent representations that are $aligned$, namely structurally similar across networks trained with different architectures, training protocols, or training datasets. We study this phenomenon in a controlled setting, where we train an ensemble of networks on regression and classification tasks using training sets perturbed by independent realizations of a noise process. We show that the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) and the training sample size influence the alignment in qualitatively similar ways in networks trained on real-world datasets and in an extremely simple $linear$ network with a single hidden layer, for which the alignment can be estimated analytically. Across linear and nonlinear networks, regression and classification tasks, and both synthetic and real-world data, we consistently observe that alignment varies monotonically with SNR but non-monotonically with training sample size. In particular, the alignment is minimized near the interpolation threshold, and a stronger alignment does not necessarily correspond to better generalization error. These findings reveal a non-trivial dependence of alignment on data quality and quantity, decoupled from generalization performance.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.26233"></a>
### [Slave-boson Formalism for Superconducting Pairing at Strong Coupling](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26233v1)

**Authors:** Sarbajit Mazumdar, Jonas Issing, Jannis Seufert, David Riegler, Peter Wölfle, Ronny Thomale, Michael Klett  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26233v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper presents a slave-boson framework to model superconductivity in the strong-coupling regime of the Hubbard model. It successfully captures the evolution of d-wave pairing and the dynamical features of the superconducting gap, providing a scalable method that bridges weak-coupling RPA and intensive numerical methods like DMFT.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a unified theoretical description of unconventional superconductivity that connects pairing symmetry, fluctuation spectra, and dynamical gap structure across broad ranges of interaction strength and doping.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates a systematic evolution of d-wave pairing symmetry and shows that spin fluctuations drive superconductivity, with the dynamical gap features qualitatively matching experimental cuprate observations.

**Method.** The authors use the spin-rotation-invariant Kotliar-Ruckenstein slave-boson approach combined with a Gaussian fluctuation expansion and a frequency-dependent Eliashberg-type gap equation.

**Model / system.** A single-band Hubbard model on a two-dimensional square lattice with nearest-neighbor (t) and next-nearest-neighbor (t') hopping, used as a proxy for cuprate physics.

**Key observables.** Spin and charge susceptibilities, superconducting gap amplitude, pairing symmetry (d-wave), critical temperature (Tc), and the real-frequency gap peak position (omega_peak).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard interaction strength (U), chemical potential (mu), doping/filling (n), temperature (T), and hopping parameter (t').

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a paramagnetic saddle point and focuses on Gaussian fluctuations; it currently lacks self-energy back-action and the inclusion of competing symmetry-broken orders.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the evolution of spin and charge susceptibilities, the n-U phase diagram for singlet superconductivity, and the frequency-dependent structure of the superconducting gap.

**Paper structure.** The paper moves from the microscopic slave-boson methodology and fluctuation derivation to the results regarding fluctuation profiles and phase diagrams, concluding with the analysis of superconducting properties and dynamical gap structure.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the emergence of superconductivity in the one-band Hubbard model using the spin-rotation-invariant Kotliar-Ruckenstein slave-boson (SB) approach. Motivated by its intrinsically renormalized mean-field ground state, we construct an effective pairing vertex from dynamical fluctuations about the saddle point. Solving the anisotropic, frequency-dependent gap equation on the square lattice, we map the pairing instabilities across doping, interaction, temperature and real-frequency gap structure that qualitatively match experimental cuprate observations. This framework merges strong-correlation SB-type renormalizations with RPA-type pairing transparency, providing a scalable route to modeling multi-orbital superconductivity at strong coupling.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.26426"></a>
### [Surface d-orbital order in intermetallic compound](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26426v1)

**Authors:** Zhanyang Hao, Haohao Sheng, Wanru Ma, Wengen Zheng, Yongqing Cai, Zijuan Xie, Wanlin Cheng, Zuowei Liang, Wu Xie, Wenjuan Zhao, Chen Liu, Zhibin Su, Junhao Lin, Liusuo Wu, Zhengtai Liu, Mao Ye, Ji Dai, Massimo Tallarida, Shengtao Cui, Yogendra Kumar, Kenya Shimada, Kenichi Ozawa, Shuki Torii, Kazuhiro Mori, Yue Xie, Junze Deng, Jiaou Wang, Xuetao Zhu, Jiandong Guo, Jiawei Mei, Zhenyu Wang, Xianhui Chen, Ping Miao, Zhijun Wang, Chaoyu Chen  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26426v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26426_figures/2605.26426_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1｜Phase diagram of Tb2CoAl4Ge2. NPD, heat capacity and susceptibility measurements reveal four</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26426_figures/2605.26426_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2｜Orientation dependent C4 symmetry breaking of surface and bulk electronic states. a, ARPES</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26426_figures/2605.26426_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 3｜Surface ferro-orbital order characterized by Fermi surface deformation, nematic orbital split and</figcaption>

**Summary.** The researchers identified a pure surface 5d-orbital order in the intermetallic compound Tb2CoAl4Ge2. This order is unique because it occurs at a temperature significantly higher than the bulk magnetic and structural transitions, providing a rare example of orbital order decoupled from lattice and spin degrees of freedom.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in identifying and distinguishing pure orbital order from concurrent structural or magnetic transitions in strongly correlated systems.

**Main result.** Discovery of a rare earth 5d-orbital order in the surface states of Tb2CoAl4Ge2 that is decoupled from bulk magnetic and structural transitions.

**Method.** A combination of ARPES, LD-ARPES, STM, and neutron powder diffraction, supported by DFT and mean-field Hamiltonian modeling.

**Model / system.** The intermetallic compound Tb2CoAl4Ge2, specifically focusing on the surface states originating from Tb 5d orbitals.

**Key observables.** Fermi surface deformation, band splitting, orbital polarization via linear dichroism, and nematic domain patterns.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Orbital ordering temperature (T ~ 51 K), magnetic transition temperatures (T_N1-T_N4), and the anisotropy energy (E_orb).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a mean-field approximation to model the ferro-orbital order and the assumption that the observed symmetry breaking is driven by orbital physics rather than structural or magnetic distortions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a phase diagram with multiple magnetic transitions; extended data figures illustrate magnetic structures, nematic domain patterns, and the insensitivity of the C2 state to magnetic fields.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of orbital order, describes the material and experimental setup, presents the discovery of surface orbital order via ARPES, provides evidence for its decoupling from bulk transitions using neutron diffraction and STM, and concludes with a theoretical validation using a mean-field Hamiltonian.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Orbital order describes a quantum state where occupied orbitals line up in a periodic pattern. While orbital physics plays a fundamental and universal role in strongly correlated electron systems, the existence and particularly the band structure fingerprint of orbital order remain a long-standing mystery. Here, we report the discovery of rare earth 5d-orbital order developed by the surface states of intermetallic compound Tb2CoAl4Ge2. Angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy reveals characteristic nematic features like Fermi surface deformation and band split. These experimental observations can be described by a ferro-orbital order term in the mean-field Hamiltonian. The structural and magnetic origin of such order is excluded by systematic high-resolution neutron powder diffraction and scanning tunnelling microscopy measurements. Our results provide strong evidence for a pure surface orbital order scenario avoiding complications from structural distortion as in colossal magnetoresistance manganites, magnetic order as in iron-based superconductors, and charge transfer p-orbital order in cuprates.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.26743"></a>
### [Thermal Casimir Effect in A Schwarzschild-like Wormhole Spacetime](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26743v1)

**Authors:** Arista Romadani, Apriadi Salim Adam, Ar Rohim, Bintoro Anang Subagyo, Agus Purwanto  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26743v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26743_figures/2605.26743_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Casimir cavity orbiting around Schwarzschild-like wormhole</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26743_figures/2605.26743_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Plot of L3</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26743_figures/2605.26743_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Plot of thermodynamics quantities: L2</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper calculates how temperature affects the Casimir effect between two plates orbiting a wormhole. It demonstrates that while the vacuum energy depends on the spacetime, the thermal corrections in the local frame are independent of the wormhole's geometry. The results are shown to be thermodynamically consistent with the Nernst heat theorem.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the finite-temperature Casimir effect and its associated thermodynamic quantities for a massless scalar field in a Schwarzschild-like wormhole spacetime.

**Main result.** The thermal correction to the renormalized Casimir free energy is found to be independent of the background geometry in the comoving frame, and the derived thermodynamic quantities are consistent with the third law of thermodynamics.

**Method.** The authors use the Matsubara formalism, Schwinger proper-time method, and Riemann zeta function regularization to compute the renormalized energy and thermodynamic quantities.

**Model / system.** A massless scalar field is confined between two parallel plates in a circular orbit within a static, zero-tidal-force, Schwarzschild-like wormhole spacetime, subject to Dirichlet boundary conditions.

**Key observables.** Renormalized Casimir free energy, Casimir entropy, internal energy, and heat capacity at constant volume.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), plate separation (L), wormhole shape function parameter (gamma), and throat radius (r0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The plates are in a comoving frame where the geometry is locally flat; the field is massless; and tidal effects inside the cavity are negligible.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the orbiting Casimir cavity; Figure 2 plots the thermal correction of the Casimir free energy versus temperature; Figure 3 shows the monotonic increase of entropy and internal energy and the peaked behavior of heat capacity.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the wormhole metric and the Casimir apparatus, describes the coordinate transformation to a comoving frame, applies regularization and renormalization techniques, analyzes the low and high-temperature limits, and discusses thermodynamic consistency.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the finite-temperature Casimir effect for a massless scalar field confined between two parallel plates in a Schwarzschild-like wormhole spacetime. Imposing Dirichlet boundary conditions, we compute the renormalized Casimir free energy in the comoving frame. We find that the thermal correction to the renormalized Casimir free energy decreases gradually with the temperature and becomes independent of the background geometry in this frame. Thermodynamic quantities derived from the Casimir free energy, namely, the renormalized Casimir entropy, internal energy, and heat capacity at constant volume, exhibit distinct temperature dependence. At low temperatures, all thermodynamic quantities recover the expected behavior, consistent with the fundamental laws of thermodynamics. These results provide a compact framework for analyzing quantum vacuum forces in gravitational backgrounds.

</details>

### [Ultrametricity of Energy Minimum Configurations of RNA Secondary Structures in the Nussinov Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26570v1)

**Authors:** A. P. Zubarev  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26570v1>

**Summary.** This paper explores how the arrangement of nucleotides determines the hierarchical, ultrametric structure of RNA folding energy landscapes. It demonstrates that sequence order can drastically shift the degree of ultrametricity, suggesting that the landscape's complexity is a tunable feature of the sequence.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the hierarchical (ultrametric) organization of the energy landscape in RNA secondary structures, specifically focusing on the ground-state configurations.

**Main result.** The degree of nontrivial ultrametricity varies widely in natural RNAs and is strongly dependent on nucleotide sequence order rather than just composition, with optimized sequences reaching extremes of 2.6% to 81%.

**Method.** Numerical study using the Nussinov algorithm for structure generation, a new direct ultrametricity criterion for triangle classification, and an adaptive random search algorithm for sequence optimization.

**Model / system.** The system consists of RNA secondary structures modeled by the Nussinov (maximum matching) model, where energy is determined by the number of base pairs, focusing on the $T=0$ degenerate ground-state manifold.

**Key observables.** Degree of nontrivial ultrametricity (u), fraction of trivial (equilateral) triangles, and the Higgs ultrametricity criterion (s/s_unc).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nucleotide composition (fixed during optimization), nucleotide sequence order, and accuracy parameters (epsilon, delta) for ultrametricity classification.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the Nussinov model's simplified combinatorial energy is sufficient to capture hierarchical properties and focuses on the $T=0$ limit without pseudoknots.

**Figures summary.** The notes do not provide specific descriptions of figures, though they mention Table 1 (natural RNA results) and Table 2 (synthetic sequence results).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of ultrametricity in RNA landscapes, presents a numerical study of natural RNAs, describes a new metric and classification criterion, details an optimization algorithm for sequence permutations, and concludes with findings on sequence-dependent hierarchy.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a numerical study of the ultrametric properties of the set of RNA secondary structures with the maximum number of base pairs (energetically degenerate minima) within the maximum matching model (Nussinov algorithm). Using 18 natural small nuclear RNAs as examples, we show that the degree of nontrivial ultrametricity varies widely. We consider the optimization problem for the degree of nontrivial ultrametricity of RNA secondary structures with the maximum number of base pairs under a fixed nucleotide composition. It is found that permuting the nucleotide sequence can strongly change the degree of ultrametricity, indicating the key role of nucleotide order in shaping the hierarchical properties of RNA secondary structures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.26779"></a>
### [Weak first-order phase transition out of the classical kagome spin liquid](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26779v1)

**Authors:** Cecilie Glittum, Olav F. Syljuåsen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26779v1>

**Summary.** This paper resolves a long-standing debate by proving that the classical kagome spin liquid undergoes a weak first-order phase transition into an ordered phase. Using an advanced self-consistent theory, the authors demonstrate that the transition is a genuine thermodynamic event rather than a smooth crossover.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the low-temperature fate of the classical kagome Heisenberg antiferromagnet and resolving whether the transition from the spin liquid to the sqrt(3)xsqrt(3) phase is a thermal crossover or a thermodynamic phase transition.

**Main result.** The study shows that the spin liquid terminates at a weak first-order phase transition into the sqrt(3)xsqrt(3) phase, where the ordered moment saturates at zero temperature.

**Method.** The authors use Nematic Bond Theory (NBT), an extension of the Self-Consistent Gaussian Approximation (SCGA) based on a large-Ns expansion, to solve for the free energy and identify phase stability.

**Model / system.** A classical Heisenberg antiferromagnet on a kagome lattice with nearest-neighbor (J1) and potentially second-neighbor (J2) interactions.

**Key observables.** Free energy, ordered moment squared (m^2_AF), specific heat (cv), spin structure factor (S(q)), correlation length (xi), and latent heat (lh).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), second-neighbor coupling (J2), number of spin components (Ns), and system size (L).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The NBT method neglects vertex corrections in the diagrammatic treatment and may overestimate critical temperatures by 10-20%.

**Figures summary.** Figures include specific heat vs. temperature for different system sizes, a J2-T phase diagram showing first-order transition lines and a triple point, real-space spin correlations, and finite-size scaling plots for latent heat and critical temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of the kagome spin liquid fate, develops the NBT mathematical framework, presents the phase diagram and transition nature, compares results with Monte Carlo simulations, and discusses the extension to J2 interactions and the pyrochlore lattice.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The low-temperature fate of the spin-liquid regime in the classical kagome Heisenberg antiferromagnet has been debated for over three decades. Using an expansion in the number of spin components, we show that, contrary to earlier Monte Carlo simulations, the spin liquid terminates at a weak first-order phase transition into the $\sqrt{3}\times\sqrt{3}$ phase which ordered moment saturates at zero temperature. Adding second-neighbor interactions, this transition belongs to a line of first-order phase transitions that ends at a critical point. For comparison, the pyrochlore antiferromagnet remains disordered at all temperatures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.27319"></a>
### [Absence of a Superradiant Phase Transition in Dirac Landau Polaritons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27319v1)

**Authors:** Elsa Jöchl, Felix Helmrich, Frieder Lindel, Lucy Hale, Lorenzo Graziotto, Mona Jarrahi, Tobia F. Nova, Jérôme Faist, Giacomo Scalari  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27319v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Dicke superradiance` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5**

**Summary.** This paper experimentally investigates the possibility of a superradiant phase transition in graphene-based Landau polaritons. By measuring the polariton dispersion in the ultrastrong coupling regime, the authors demonstrate that the system follows the Hopfield model and lacks the mode softening predicted by the Dicke model. This result effectively rules out a No-Go-evading phase transition in these graphene systems at current experimental scales.

**Why it may be interesting.** It addresses a fundamental debate in cavity QED regarding the existence of equilibrium phase transitions in light-matter systems and provides experimental evidence for the role of the diamagnetic term in the ultrastrong coupling regime.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether a superradiant phase transition (SRPT) can occur in graphene Landau polaritons, specifically testing if the system evades the 'No-Go' theorem that suppresses such transitions due to the diamagnetic A^2 term.

**Main result.** The experiment rules out a No-Go-evading SRPT in graphene Landau polaritons at accessible coupling strengths, finding that the system follows the Hopfield model rather than the Dicke model.

**Method.** THz time-domain spectroscopy was used on an encapsulated monolayer graphene flake coupled to a split-ring resonator, with carrier density tuned via electrostatic gating and magnetic fields applied to induce Landau levels.

**Model / system.** The system consists of an encapsulated monolayer graphene flake ultrastrongly coupled to a single complementary split-ring resonator (cSRR). The physics is analyzed by comparing the Dicke model (which predicts SRPT) against the Hopfield Hamiltonian (which includes the diamagnetic A^2 term).

**Key observables.** Upper and lower polariton branches, polariton splitting, cyclotron frequencies, and THz transmission spectra.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Normalized coupling strength (up to Omega_R/omega_cav approx 0.4), carrier density (n), magnetic field (B), and cyclotron mass (m*).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the near-field model for the sub-wavelength cavity and is limited by the experimental ability to reach the predicted critical density for the phase transition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares Dicke vs. Hopfield model predictions; Figure 2 shows spectral maps of polariton evolution; Figure 4 shows the UP frequency blue-shift (confirming Hopfield) and the lack of LP mode softening (ruling out SRPT).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SRPT debate and the No-Go theorem, describes the graphene-cSRR experimental platform, presents THz spectroscopic results showing polariton dispersion, compares these results to Dicke and Hopfield models, and concludes with the implications for vacuum-induced order.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The condensation of photons into a macroscopically populated ground state, a superradiant phase transition (SRPT), is one of the most striking predictions of cavity quantum electrodynamics (cQED), yet has resisted experimental realization in equilibrium for over fifty years. Whether such a transition can survive at all in light-matter coupled systems is still debated, with a widely established \textit{No-Go} theorem ruling it out across several models. Graphene cyclotron transitions ultrastrongly coupled to terahertz (THz) cavities have been at the heart of this debate. At leading order, the linear Dirac dispersion of electrons does not generate the diamagnetic $\vec{A}^2$ term that enforces the theorem, making graphene the cleanest candidate for a No-Go-evading phase transition.   Here, we present the first THz spectroscopic measurements of an encapsulated monolayer graphene flake ultrastrongly coupled to a single complementary split-ring resonator. By tuning the carrier density we sweep the system from weak coupling into the ultrastrong regime, reaching a normalized coupling $Ω_\text{R}/ω_\text{cav} \approx 0.4$. This is well into the range where a second order SRPT would manifest by a softening of the lower polariton branch, which we do not observe. The full polariton dispersion is instead quantitatively reproduced by a Hopfield Hamiltonian derived from first principles using a near-field model that accounts for the sub-wavelength character of the cavity.   Our results establish an experimental baseline for predictions of cavity-driven phase transitions in two-dimensional Dirac systems, and rule out a No-Go-evading SRPT in graphene Landau polaritons up to the strongest couplings accessible today, with direct implications for proposals invoking vacuum-induced order in solid-state cQED.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.26319"></a>
### [Programming nanomechanical computation with light](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26319v1)

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** The researchers have created a programmable computing platform using nanomechanical resonators where the logic functions and connections are controlled by light. By tuning lasers, they can switch between different Boolean gates and even connect different mechanical modes. This work shows that optomechanical nonlinearities can be harnessed to create reconfigurable, hardware-agnostic physical computers.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a way to use classical nonlinear dynamics and optomechanical backaction to implement programmable logic, which provides a bridge to understanding how controlled nonlinearities can be used for more complex tasks like reservoir computing or probabilistic computing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a programmable physical computing platform where nonlinearity and connectivity can be reconfigured in situ, rather than being fixed by material properties.

**Main result.** Demonstration of all six fundamental Boolean logic gates (AND, NAND, OR, NOR, XOR, XNOR) and programmable connectivity between mechanical modes using laser-controlled optomechanical interactions.

**Method.** Using cavity optomechanical backaction to induce Duffing nonlinearity and an optical spring effect, controlled via laser wavelength, power, and phase, with readout via intensity modulation detection.

**Model / system.** A pair of silicon nanobeams acting as mechanical oscillators coupled to a photonic-crystal nanocavity, modeled as a Duffing oscillator with radiation-pressure-induced nonlinearity.

**Key observables.** Mechanical oscillation amplitude, frequency response (Lorentzian vs. shark-fin), power spectral density (PSD), and gate fidelity/error rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mechanical frequency (5-18 MHz), optical detuning, Duffing nonlinearity coefficient (beta), damping rate (Gamma), and thermal amplitude (x_th).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Use of the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA) for single-mode treatment and neglecting higher-order harmonics and intermodulation products through optimized DC bias.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental setup and the transition from linear to nonlinear frequency response. Figure 2 demonstrates the realization of all six logic gates. Figure 3 shows the implementation of cascadable gates using multiple eigenmodes. Figure 4 shows the time trace and noise analysis of an OR gate.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of programmable physical computing, describes the optomechanical platform and the Duffing model, demonstrates the implementation of various logic gates, shows the extension to multi-mode cascading, and concludes with a detailed noise and error rate analysis.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Looking at physical systems as computers allows us to regard physical properties, such as thermal noise, symmetry or topology, as unconventional resources for computation. However, harnessing these resources requires programming computational functionality through strong, controllable nonlinearities in the system. Here, we show that cavity optomechanical interactions allow laser-controlled computation with nanomechanical degrees of freedom. We demonstrate a set of basic digital logic gates with level restoration and controlled mechanical couplings as essential ingredients for arbitrary computing networks. Owing to the strong optomechanical nonlinearity and precise readout, the system operates close to thermal amplitudes, in the regime where thermodynamic stochasticity governs its behavior. This opens a new path for the realization of physical computing with controlled nonlinear resonators.

</details>

### [High-mobility inertial domain walls driven by spin-transfer torque in a ferrimagnetic spinel oxide](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27216v1)

**Authors:** Mingxing Wu, Shilei Ding, Laura van Schie, Shenghao Cai, Yuhao Qiu, Ao Du, Alexander E. Kossak, Rui Wu, Christian L. Degen, Xuegang Chen, Pietro Gambardella

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of achieving high-velocity, energy-efficient domain wall motion in magnetic devices for spintronic applications like racetrack memories.

**Main result.** Demonstrated domain wall velocities exceeding 1 km/s in NiCo2O4 with high mobility and a pronounced inertial effect due to large nonadiabatic spin-transfer torque.

**Method.** Used epitaxial growth of NiCo2O4 thin films, scanning NV magnetometry for domain wall structure, and MOKE microscopy for tracking dynamics under current pulses.

**Model / system.** Single-layer ferrimagnetic spinel oxide NiCo2O4 (NCO) thin films grown on MgAl2O4 substrates, utilizing a 1D domain wall model to describe dynamics.

**Key observables.** Domain wall velocity, domain wall width, chirality, nonadiabatic torque parameter (beta/alpha), and energy consumption per volume.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Current density (2 x 10^11 A/m2), characteristic relaxation time (~1 ns), and spin polarization (-0.73).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes spin-transfer torque is the sole driving mechanism and that the system operates in a regime without Walker breakdown.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Structural and magnetic characterization (XRD, STEM, SQUID). Fig 2: NV microscopy of domain wall structure. Fig 3: Current-driven domain wall displacement and velocity. Fig 4: Evidence of domain wall inertia. Fig 5: Comparative performance of NCO against other magnetic materials.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with material characterization, moves to structural analysis of the domain wall, presents dynamic measurements of velocity and inertia, and concludes with a comparative performance analysis against other magnetic systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Efficient electrical manipulation of domain walls is key to developing magnetic devices with fast switching capabilities and low energy consumption. Here we demonstrate Bloch-type domain wall velocities exceeding 1 km s$^{-1}$ in the single-layer ferrimagnetic spinel oxide NiCo$_2$O$_4$ induced by spin-transfer torque at a current density of $2 \times 10^{11}$ A m$^{-2}$. This exceptional domain wall mobility is attributed to the combination of giant nonadiabatic spin-transfer torque, low magnetization, and high spin polarization. Additionally, we report a pronounced domain wall inertia effect in this ferrimagnet due to the large nonadiabaticity of the torque. The characteristic time for domain wall acceleration and deceleration is $\sim 1$ ns, shorter than that reported for typical ferromagnets. Our findings highlight the potential of spinel oxides as a promising platform for engineering high-performance domain wall devices that take advantage of ultrafast ferrimagnetic dynamics.

</details>

### [Anharmonic Quantum Transport Analysis of Thermal Transport Anomalies in Ultrathin Silicon Nanowires](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26529v1)

**Authors:** Lokanath Patra, Mayur Pratap Singh, Satish Kumar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26529v1>

**Summary.** This paper uses a quantum-mechanical NEGF framework with machine-learning potentials to explain why thermal conductivity in ultrathin silicon nanowires does not decrease linearly with diameter. It identifies a critical diameter where thermal transport transitions between hydrodynamic-like phonon flow and a regime dominated by quantum confinement and boundary scattering.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the origin of the nonmonotonic dependence of thermal conductivity on the diameter in ultrathin silicon nanowires and resolving discrepancies between classical MD and quantum-mechanical reality.

**Main result.** Confirmed a nonmonotonic thermal conductivity behavior where a minimum occurs at critical diameters (6.24 nm for [001] and 5.50 nm for [110]), driven by the competition between boundary scattering and hydrodynamic-like phonon flow at room temperature, and radial quantum confinement at cryogenic temperatures.

**Method.** Anharmonic non-equilibrium Green's function (NEGF) simulations combined with neuroevolution potentials (NEP) trained on density-functional-theory (DFT) data.

**Model / system.** Ultrathin hydrogen-passivated silicon nanowires (Si NWs) with diameters ranging from 0.5 to 6 nm, oriented along [001] and [110] crystallographic directions.

**Key observables.** Thermal conductivity (k), spectral thermal conductance, phonon scattering rates (Normal vs. Umklapp), and phonon dispersion/band structures.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nanowire diameter (d), temperature (10 K to 300 K), and crystallographic orientation ([001] and [110]).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The NW length is fixed at 10 nm; the Cauchy principal integral in the retarded self-energy calculation is neglected to reduce complexity; Gaussian broadening is used for numerical smoothing.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the NW structures and phonon band structure agreement; Figure 2 plots thermal conductivity vs. diameter at 10 K and 300 K; Figure 5 displays frequency-resolved spectral thermal conductance for various diameters and temperatures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the thermal transport problem, describes the ML-NEGF/NEP methodology, presents results for thermal conductivity across different diameters and orientations, analyzes the underlying phonon scattering mechanisms at different temperature regimes, and provides supplementary details on potential training and spectral conductance.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Thermal transport in low-dimensional semiconductors is crucial for advancing thermal management in nanoelectronics, quantum devices, and thermoelectric devices. Recent molecular dynamics (MD) studies have identified a nonmonotonic dependence of thermal conductivity (k) on diameter in ultrathin silicon nanowires (NWs). However, classical MD methods are limited at low temperatures and in strongly confined regimes. This work introduces a fully quantum-mechanical perspective on this anomaly by employing anharmonic non-equilibrium Green's function (NEGF) simulations combined with density-functional-theory-trained neuroevolution potentials. For [001]- and [110]-oriented NWs, k decreases with diameter d to a minimum at d_c = 6.24 nm and 5.50 nm, respectively, then rises with d, for a temperature range of 10-300 K. At room temperature, this behavior arises from dominant momentum-conserving normal scattering relative to Umklapp processes in confined regimes, thereby enabling Poiseuille-like hydrodynamic phonon flow that competes with boundary scattering. At cryogenic temperatures, strong radial confinement quantizes the phonon spectrum, and only low-frequency phonons (< 2 THz) significantly contribute to heat transport through quasi-ballistic propagation of long-wavelength modes, as demonstrated by the spectral thermal conductance. In contrast to classical MD, which is inaccurate at low temperatures due to overexcitation of high-frequency vibrations by Boltzmann statistics, neglect of quantum suppression, and overestimation of thermal conductivity in thinner NWs with stronger quantum confinement, the NEGF framework provides quantitative accuracy even at low temperatures, such as 10 K.

</details>

### [Anomaly-Induced Hybrid Bulk Electromagnetic Mode in Weyl Semimetals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27031v1)

**Authors:** Subrahmanyam D, Suhas Gangadharaiah, E. G. Mishchenko  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27031v1>

**Summary.** This paper predicts a new type of bulk electromagnetic excitation in Weyl semimetals that occurs due to the chiral anomaly. This hybrid mode is distinct from known excitations and can be identified through its unique dispersion and avoided crossing with the bulk plasmon. The discovery offers a new way to probe the chiral magnetic effect using electron energy-loss spectroscopy.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The identification and characterization of a previously unknown hybrid bulk electromagnetic mode in Weyl semimetals arising from the interplay between the chiral anomaly and the orientation of the chiral magnetic response.

**Main result.** The authors predict a linearly dispersing hybrid mode that undergoes an avoided crossing with the bulk plasmon when the chiral magnetic current has a component along the wave-vector, providing a direct signature of the chiral anomaly.

**Method.** A semiclassical kinetic-Maxwell framework using the Boltzmann formalism and the continuity equation to solve coupled kinetic and Maxwell equations.

**Model / system.** Doped 3D Weyl semimetals with broken time-reversal symmetry, characterized by a node-separation vector and a weak static external magnetic field.

**Key observables.** Loss function spectra L(omega, q), dispersion relations, and damping rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Orientation angle theta between the magnetic field and wave-vector, wave-vector q, frequency scale omega_anom, and the ratio of internode to intra-node scattering rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Long-wavelength regime, collisionless limit, negligible Landau quantization, and neglecting interband effects due to low Fermi energies.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of the system; Figure 2 shows loss-function spectra L(omega, q) for different magnetic field configurations and angles, highlighting the presence or absence of the hybrid mode.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of anomaly-induced modes, describes the semiclassical kinetic-Maxwell model, analyzes the electromagnetic response in different magnetic field orientations (parallel vs. perpendicular), and concludes with the predicted experimental signatures in EELS.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Collective modes provide direct fingerprints of quantum matter. We predict a previously unidentified hybrid bulk electromagnetic mode in Weyl semimetals arising from the interplay between the chiral anomaly and the orientation of its associated chiral magnetic response relative to the direction of the wave-vector. When the anomaly-induced chiral magnetic current has a component along the propagation direction, oscillations of valley imbalance hybridize with plasmonic charge oscillations, producing a linearly dispersing mode that undergoes avoided crossing with the bulk plasmon, producing a hybrid bulk excitation absent in ordinary metals. The hybrid mode provides a direct signature of Weyl semimetals and a probe of the chiral anomaly and its associated chiral magnetic effect, with observable features in electron energy-loss spectroscopy. Studying this interplay can uncover various optical and electronic properties of Weyl semimetals.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.26811"></a>
### [Interaction-Phase Dynamics and Spectral Organization in Damped Higher-Order Nonlinear Schrödinger Models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26811v1)

**Authors:** C. M. Schober  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26811v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper explains how different types of dissipation fundamentally alter the stability of nonlinear wave structures. It shows that while viscous damping destroys spectral organization, nonlinear mean-flow damping allows for complex phase restructuring without breaking the underlying coherent Floquet-band structure.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates why two different dissipative mechanisms—viscous damping and nonlinear mean-flow damping—lead to fundamentally different long-time Floquet spectral evolutions and modulation dynamics in higher-order nonlinear Schrödinger (HONLS) systems.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that nonlinear mean-flow damping maintains organized, recurrent Floquet-band structures through interaction-dependent dissipative feedback, whereas viscous damping causes spectral reconnection, diffusion, and the breakdown of coherent modulation.

**Method.** The research employs a reduced five-mode Fourier truncation and an amplitude-phase reformulation to isolate interaction phases, alongside numerical simulations and comparisons with integrable NLS finite-gap benchmark solutions.

**Model / system.** The system consists of Higher-Order Nonlinear Schrödinger (HONLS) models subject to two distinct dissipative extensions: Viscous-HONLS (V-HONLS) and Nonlinear Mean-Flow Damped-HONLS (NLD-HONLS).

**Key observables.** Nonlinear Floquet spectrum (band structure, critical points, and reconnection), interaction phases (psi_j), wave amplitude evolution, and peak wavenumber (k_peak).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Viscous damping strength (Gamma), nonlinear mean-flow damping parameter (beta), higher-order dispersive correction (epsilon), and wavenumber (mu).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on a five-mode Fourier truncation and assumes a carrier-sideband hierarchy (r_0 >> r_plus/minus_1 >> r_plus/minus_2) for asymptotic reductions.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the evolution of Floquet spectra over time, comparison of surface amplitude and wavenumber dynamics, and the evolution of interaction phases correlated with rogue-wave events and spectral restructuring.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the HONLS models and Floquet diagnostic framework, moves to the derivation of a reduced five-mode interaction-phase model, presents numerical comparisons between viscous and mean-flow damping, and concludes with benchmark analyses using integrable NLS solutions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the dynamical mechanisms underlying the contrasting nonlinear Floquet spectral evolutions observed in viscous and nonlinear mean-flow damped higher-order nonlinear Schr"odinger systems. Motivated by the persistent organized Floquet-band structure under nonlinear mean-flow damping and the repeated Floquet band reconnection observed under viscous damping, we derive a reduced five-mode carrier-sideband truncation and reformulate the dynamics in amplitude-phase variables to isolate the dominant interaction phases associated with the principal four-wave interaction products. Within this framework, viscous damping acts primarily modewise and does not directly modify the leading interaction-phase dynamics. By contrast, nonlinear mean-flow damping contributes directly to the interaction-phase evolution through interaction-dependent dissipative corrections. In the carrier-sideband regime, these corrections generate terms of the form $-κ_j \sin(ψ_j)$, introducing dissipative feedback into the dominant carrier-sideband interaction dynamics. To interpret the resulting interaction-phase evolution, we examine recurrent finite-gap NLS benchmark solutions whose modulation dynamics are independently understood. These benchmarks show that substantial interaction-phase evolution and localized restructuring may occur even within organized quasiperiodic dynamics possessing invariant Floquet spectral structure. Numerical diagnostics show that the nonlinear mean-flow damped system exhibits persistent recurrent carrier-sideband focusing dynamics together with organized Floquet evolution despite substantial interaction-phase restructuring, whereas the viscous system exhibits progressively diffuse modulation dynamics together with repeated Floquet reconfiguration and weakening persistence of the recurrent carrier-sideband interaction structure.

</details>

### [Amorphous vs. Short-Range-Ordered Complexions: Consequences for Grain-Boundary-Mediated Plasticity in Nanocrystalline Al-Ni Alloys](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27218v1)

**Authors:** Frederic Sansoz, Eve-Audrey Picard  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27218v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores how the structural state of grain boundaries—either purely amorphous or containing short-range order—dictates the mechanical properties of nanocrystalline Al-Ni alloys. It demonstrates that while SRO increases material strength, it also triggers detrimental shear localization. These findings suggest that controlling the structural order at interfaces can be used to engineer the plasticity of nanostructured materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how local short-range ordering (SRO) within amorphous grain-boundary complexions influences the plasticity and mechanical response of nanocrystalline Al-Ni alloys.

**Main result.** Amorphous grain boundaries promote homogeneous plasticity via shear transformation zones but lower strength, whereas SRO-containing boundaries increase strength but induce intense shear localization due to stress heterogeneity.

**Method.** Large-scale hybrid Monte Carlo and Molecular Dynamics (MC/MD) simulations using the LAMMPS framework and an EAM interatomic potential.

**Model / system.** Nanocrystalline Al-Ni alloys (Al-2Ni and Al-4Ni) consisting of an FCC Al polycrystal with approximately 13 million atoms and an average grain size of 24 nm.

**Key observables.** Stress-strain curves, grain-boundary thickness, radial distribution functions (RDF), polyhedral template matching (PTM) for structural ordering, and atomic-scale von Mises stress and strain distributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ni concentrations (2 at.% and 4 at.%), annealing temperatures (378 K and 913 K), and engineering strain rate (10^8 s^-1).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The interatomic interactions are accurately captured by the EAM potential, and the grain boundary complexions reach equilibrium via the simulated annealing regimes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the equilibrium microstructure and Ni segregation; Figure 2 presents the Al-Ni phase diagram; Figure 3 compares Ni-Ni RDF curves; Figure 4 visualizes the structural difference between amorphous and SRO complexions; Figures 5-6 show tensile behavior and shear strain; Figures 7-8 compare atomic-scale von Mises stress distributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of GB-mediated plasticity, describes the MC/MD simulation setup and structural analysis tools, presents the structural evolution of GBs under different annealing temperatures, compares the mechanical response (strength vs. localization) of amorphous and SRO regimes, and concludes with implications for complexion engineering.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Amorphous grain-boundary (GB) complexions in thermally stable nanocrystalline alloys are commonly assumed to be structurally homogeneous, yet their disordered nature makes them susceptible to local short-range ordering (SRO). The influence of local SRO on GB-mediated plasticity mechanisms in such complexions remains poorly understood. This article employs large-scale Monte Carlo and molecular dynamics simulation to address this gap through simulations of nanocrystalline Al-Ni alloys at two Ni concentrations, 2 at.% and 4 at.%. Annealing at 913 K produces thick uniform amorphous intergranular film complexions, while annealing at 378 K produces semi-amorphous complexions containing FCC-type and BCC-type SRO. These two complexion states produce fundamentally different mechanical responses. Amorphous complexions act as dislocation sinks, suppressing shear localization and promoting homogeneous plasticity through shear transformation zones, but at the cost of lower strength. SRO complexions generate higher strength but also promote heterogeneous stress concentrations across the GB network, leading to intense shear localization regardless of Ni concentration. This contrast reflects a fundamental shift in governing mechanism, from shear-transformation-zone-controlled behavior in amorphous complexion alloys to GB-stress-heterogeneity-controlled behavior in SRO complexion alloys. These findings highlight the potential of complexion engineering to tailor the mechanical properties of nanocrystalline materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27099"></a>
### [Antisymmetric spontaneous resistivity anisotropy due to hard-axis collapse in polycrystalline Co thin films](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27099v1)

**Authors:** Y. Fernandes, J. Geshev, A. M. H. de Andrade, A. D. C. Viegas  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27099v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.27099_figures/2605.27099_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Panel (e) illustrates a possible device concept for creating remanent states using simple quasi-coplanar perpendicular current channels.</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that applying a magnetic field to polycrystalline Co thin films can create stable, multi-level magnetic states due to hard-axis collapse. These states are detectable via the planar Hall effect, offering a simple and low-cost platform for developing next-generation magnetic memory and sensors.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the magnetoresistance phenomena associated with hard-axis collapse (HAC) in polycrystalline Co thin films and exploring its potential for multi-level magnetic memory and sensing.

**Main result.** The study identifies at least three stable, reproducible remanent resistance states in the planar Hall resistance, originating from non-uniform magnetic configurations, which could enable low-cost spintronic devices.

**Method.** DC magnetron sputtering was used for film growth, followed by XRD for structural characterization, VSM for vector magnetometry, and four-point probe transport measurements at room temperature.

**Model / system.** Polycrystalline Cobalt (Co) thin films (20 nm) grown on Si(100) substrates with various underlayers (Ta, TiO2, Al2O3) and induced in-plane uniaxial magnetic anisotropy.

**Key observables.** Longitudinal AMR (LAMR), Transverse AMR/Planar Hall Effect (TAMR/PHE), remanent magnetization (M_rem), and anisotropy field (Ha).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Anisotropy field (30-42 Oe), field angle (alpha), current density angle (phi_j), and remanent states (chi_1, chi_2, chi_3).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The behavior is assumed to be robust across different thicknesses (5 to 150 nm) and structural variations.

**Figures summary.** XRD patterns showing crystallinity; angular variations of remanent magnetization; LAMR data at saturation and remanence; and the antisymmetric TAMR behavior near the hard axis.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the HAC phenomenon, details the experimental fabrication and structural characterization, presents transport and magnetometry results, discusses the physical mechanism of TAMR via spin-orbit coupling, and concludes with potential applications in spintronics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate magnetoresistance phenomena associated with the magnetization hard-axis collapse in polycrystalline Co thin films. Transport measurements reveal that, for specific orientations of the applied magnetic field, the system exhibits distinct remanent resistance levels in both the in-plane longitudinal and transverse voltage responses. In particular, the planar Hall resistance shows multiple stable and reproducible levels at room temperature, enabling the identification of at least three remanent states that can be distinguished and used for information storage. These resistance levels originate from non-uniform magnetic configurations stabilized after the application and removal of the external magnetic field in the hard-axis region. Since this phenomenon remains largely unexplored, we present an incipient study addressing its potential implications from an applied-physics perspective. The observation of such behavior in polycrystalline Co thin films grown on Si substrates suggests a simple and low-cost platform for spintronic memory and sensing devices based on the remanent planar Hall effect.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.26536"></a>
### [Conditions for domain-free negative capacitance](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26536v1)

**Authors:** Prasanna Venkatesan Ravindran, Priyankka Gundlapudi Ravikumar, Asif Islam Khan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26536v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26536_figures/2605.26536_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Phase plot of effective Curie temperature as a function of the thick- ness ratio r: The black curve shows the transition from a homogeneous zero- polarization, negative capacitance state to a multi-domain ferroelectric state in a FE-DE heterostructure. Despite several experimental efforts, the former has re- mained elusive leading to questions about the existence of such an ideal negative capacitance state. This letter analyzes the conditions on the domain wall energy parameter D for which the zero-polarization state becomes energetically more favorable than the multi-domain state at a given operating temperature. TC0 is the Curie temperature of the standalone ferroelectric...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26536_figures/2605.26536_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: The variation of effective Curie temperature (a) with thickness ra- tios for different D and (b) with domain wall energy parameters for different r in FE-DE heterostructures with tF = 5 nm. As D is increased, the effec- tive Curie temperatures of multidomain heterostructures(blue) approach the effective Curie temperatures calculated from the single domain, homogeneous polarization model(red).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.26536_figures/2605.26536_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: For a given tF &lt; tF,C there exists a critical domain wall energy parameter DC above which the system is in a stable zero-polarization negative capacitance state at an operating temperature of 300 K and for tD = 5 nm. While D is an intrinsic property, the critical domain wall energy parameter DC depends on the thickness of the ferroelectric and is not an intrinsic property.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper identifies that controlling domain wall energy is key to achieving stable, domain-free negative capacitance in ferroelectric heterostructures. It provides a theoretical threshold (Dc) above which the system avoids the multi-domain patterns typically seen in experiments. This finding suggests that engineering high domain wall energy is a viable path for optimizing negative capacitance devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the specific conditions, particularly regarding domain wall energy, required to achieve an ideal, domain-free negative capacitance state in ferroelectric-dielectric heterostructures.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a stable, domain-free negative capacitance state is achieved if the domain wall energy parameter (D) exceeds a critical value (Dc), effectively suppressing domain formation.

**Method.** The study uses a Landau-Ginzburg-Devonshire framework and a non-linear Ginzburg-Landau equation to analytically and numerically compare single-domain and multi-domain phase transitions.

**Model / system.** The system consists of ferroelectric-dielectric (FE-DE) heterostructures, specifically modeled after PbTiO3/SrTiO3 superlattices, using Landau-Devonshire theory to describe polarization dynamics.

**Key observables.** Effective Curie temperature (T'C), polarization (P), and the domain wall energy parameter (D).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Domain wall energy parameter (D), critical ferroelectric thickness (tf,c), thickness ratio (r), and operating temperature (Top).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes the system is near the Curie temperature and relies on a dimensionless parameter to represent domain wall energy costs; it also notes limitations in the current database of domain wall energies.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2(b) shows the variation of T'C with D for different dielectric thicknesses, and Figure 3 illustrates the variation of the critical domain wall energy parameter Dc with ferroelectric thickness.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of domain formation in negative capacitance, presents a multi-domain model using Ginzburg-Landau theory, derives the critical conditions for a domain-free state, discusses the implications for specific materials like HfO2, and concludes with future directions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

While negative capacitance has been demonstrated in ferroelectric-dielectric heterostructures in the form of capacitance enhancement, all experimental evidence, to date, suggests the existence of domains therein. Here, we address the question: what are the conditions to achieve ideal, domain-free negative capacitance in ferroelectric-dielectric heterostructures? Our main claim is that for given thicknesses of the ferroelectric and the dielectric layers, there is a critical value of domain wall energy parameter -- above which the system would be stabilized in an ideal and robust domain-free negative capacitance state and would be robust against domain formation. Our analyses suggest that to achieve ideal negative capacitance, efforts should lie in understanding the means to control the domain wall energy on all fronts, both theory and experiments via high throughput design, discovery, and engineering of ferroelectrics.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses computational methods to predict new stable potassium-carbon compounds under high pressure. It identifies a specific phase of potassium carbide (Imma KC) that could serve as a promising superconductor at relatively low pressures (25 GPa) with a Tc of 21.4 K.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to systematically investigate the structural stability, chemical bonding, and superconducting properties of the binary potassium-carbon (K-C) system under high pressure.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered several new stable K-C phases, most notably an orthorhombic Imma-KC phase that reaches a high superconducting transition temperature (Tc) of 21.4 K at 25 GPa.

**Method.** The study uses the CALYPSO swarm intelligence technique for structure prediction combined with Density Functional Theory (DFT) and the Allen-Dynes modified McMillan equation for superconductivity calculations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a binary potassium-carbon (K-C) system under high pressure (up to 300 GPa), encompassing various stoichiometries such as K7C, KC, and KC3.

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature (Tc), electron-phonon coupling constant (lambda), phonon dispersion curves, and electron localization function (ELF).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pressure ranges from 1 atm to 300 GPa; Coulomb pseudopotential (mu*) set to 0.1.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study relies on the accuracy of the PBE functional within the GGA approximation and assumes the validity of the BCS theory for superconductivity.

**Figures summary.** Figures include electronic band structures, projected density of states (PDOS), phonon dispersion curves, and phonon projected density of states (PHDOS).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the research motivation, describes the computational methods (CALYPSO and DFT), presents the structural and thermodynamic stability results, analyzes the electronic and superconducting properties of specific phases, and concludes with the implications for metal carbide superconductors.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Carbon possesses remarkable physicochemical properties, enabling main group metals to react with it under high pressure and produce superconducting compounds. However, the research on high-pressure potassium carbides remains still unsystematic and inadequate. Using first-principles calculations and state-of-the-art swarm intelligence structure prediction techniques, we comprehensively explore the binary novel potassium-carbon system and characterize their structural, electronic, and superconducting properties under compression. Calculations of electron-phonon coupling show that monoclinic K7C (space group C2/m) with high K concentration is a zero-dimensional electride superconductor with a transition temperature Tc of 0.6 K at 25 GPa. By contrast, the Tc of orthorhombic KC with symmetry Imma increases upon decompression owing to the phonon softening of both acoustic and optical branches. Impressively, Imma KC reaches a maximum Tc of 21.4 K at 25 GPa, which is highly desirable for low-pressure metallic carbide superconductors. This work provides valuable insights into K-C compounds and broadens the diversity of metal carbide superconductors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.26393"></a>
### [Locally resolved electronic textures of reconstruction domains in marginally twisted monolayer-bilayer graphene](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.26393v1)

**Authors:** Sean M. Walker, Patrick Sarsfield, Isaac Soltero, Xue-Ying LiYang, Laurent Molino, Ryan Plumadore, Kenji Watanabe, Takashi Taniguchi, Vladimir Falko, Adina Luican-Mayer  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.26393v1>
